A</a></span>, 2024, <strong>12</strong>, 21176-21189</div><h1 id="sect191"><span class="title_heading">Structural design of biomass-derived hard carbon anode materials for superior sodium storage <span class="italic">via</span> increasing crystalline cellulose and closing the open pores<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">

    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      11th May 2024
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 3rd July 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 4th July 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Biomass-derived hard carbon is a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries. However, ensuring the consistency of hard carbon poses a challenge due to the intricate properties of biomass precursors, impacting their large-scale and stable supply. In this study, we used a two-step acid treatment and a two-stage heat treatment process to modulate the properties of the biomass precursor and design the structure of hard carbon materials. During the acid treatment, the removal of both the amorphous region of cellulose and hemicellulose fosters the formation of additional graphite microcrystals while concurrently decreasing the specific surface area of the resulting hard carbon. Moreover, adjustments in the pre-carbonization conditions lead to the enlargement of the carbon interlayer spacing, reduction of open pores, and the introduction of more oxygen-containing groups into the carbon structure. As a result, the optimized sample demonstrates an impressive specific capacity of 342.4 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 87.19% at 30 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, sustaining a reversible capacity of 128.3 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 10 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Additionally, the sodium storage mechanism of the resulting hard carbon material was confirmed to be adsorption-intercalation-filling by kinetics and structural evolution analysis. This work sheds light on the effective obtaining of biomass-derived hard carbon for enhanced sodium ion storage.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect278"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>In recent years, adjusting the energy structure to achieve sustainable development has gradually become a consensus among people. Replacing traditional fossil fuels with renewable energy is considered an effective way. Since renewable energy sources such as wind and solar have fluctuating characteristics, energy storage systems (ESSs) must be equipped in practical applications to overcome the time domain limitations and relieve the pressure on the power grid.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Lithium-ion batteries (LiBs) are currently the most widely used electrochemical energy storage devices, but the abundance of lithium in the earth's crust is only about 20 ppm. As the scale of its application continues to expand, resource reserves and costs of lithium will gradually become the key factors limiting the development of LiBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,3</span></sup></a> Sodium-ion batteries (SiBs), with working principles similar to LiBs, have been widely accepted as applicable complements to LiBs for large-scale energy storage and low-speed vehicles due to their resource and cost advantages.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> As the battery's core, the electrode material directly affects the operating voltage, capacity, and other battery performance. Drawing on the research experience of LiBs, the cathode side has made significant progress.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3,5</span></sup></a> However, the commercial graphite anode exhibits low capacity in SiBs due to its features (small layer spacing and unstable NaC<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6,7</span></sup></a> Therefore, developing low-cost, easy-to-prepare, and high-performance anode materials is of great significance.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">Among all reported anode materials, hard carbon materials with high specific capacity (about 300 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), low operating voltage (about 0.2 V), long cycle life, and high cost-effectiveness<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8,9</span></sup></a> are regarded as one of the most promising anode materials for SiBs. The precursors of hard carbon include organic compounds and polymers such as starch,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> sucrose,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> and resin<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12,13</span></sup></a> as well as various biomass raw materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1419</span></sup></a> The pyrolysis carbon yield of the above precursors is generally below 50%. The resin has a relatively high carbon yield of 40% but is also more expensive. The carbon yield of starch and sucrose is less than 10%.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20</span></sup></a> The high price and low carbon yield of precursors will significantly increase the production cost of hard carbon materials, affecting the cost advantage of SiBs. Compared to organic polymers, biomass raw materials are ubiquitous, and their low price and easy availability make them one of the ideal precursors of hard carbon.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21,22</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Despite its cost advantage, the complex chemical composition and unstable supply of biomass raw materials will adversely affect their electrochemical performance, thus posing challenges to large-scale production and application. Preparing biomass-derived hard carbon generally involves two steps: pre-treatment and carbonization. Take lignocellulosic biomass as an example; the three main components of cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin are composed of different subunits and have different physical and chemical properties. Lignin and hemicellulose are two amorphous components that are difficult to graphitize,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> while cellulose with high crystallinity will retain its regularly arranged skeleton structure during pyrolysis, the degree of graphitization is relatively high.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> Furthermore, cellulose and hemicellulose contain abundant hydroxyl groups, and the thermal decomposition of these two components will produce micropores. Pyrolysis of lignin composed of aromatic <span class="italic">p</span>-hydroxyphenyl, guaiacyl, and syringyl units tends to make nonporous carbon materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24,25</span></sup></a> Therefore, cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin contents must reach a certain balance to ensure the acquisition of hard carbon with a turbostratic layered structure (graphitic-like nanodomains and highly disordered regions coexist).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> To control the composition of precursors, Zhou<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a><span class="italic">et al.</span> used NaClO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, NaOH, and HCl to chemically pretreat rosewood, a hard carbon material with a rich closed-pore structure was obtained. Although this method can effectively reduce lignin and hemicellulose content, the reaction conditions are challenging to control; both the alkali and acid treatment are required, and the process is relatively tedious. Carbonization condition is another critical factor that determines the physicochemical properties of hard carbon materials alongside the pretreatment steps. Parameters like carbonization temperature, heating rate, gas flow rate, and carbonization steps exert profound influence on the structural characteristics of hard carbon.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2832</span></sup></a> Alvin<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a><span class="italic">et al.</span> used fir wood as a precursor to study the effect of two-step carbonization. The pre-carbonization step under low-temperature conditions provides more time for carbon atoms to reorganize and develop a graphitic-like structure. The resulting hard carbon anode exhibits higher reversible capacity and better rate performance with increased graphitization. Previous studies have predominantly focused on a one-step carbonization pathway. Though a few have delved into the two-step heat treatment process, the influence of conditions such as heating rate and residence temperature of pre-carbonization remains to be explored. Therefore, it is crucial to develop a structural design strategy to obtain biomass-derived hard carbon materials facilely and elucidate the intrinsic connection among properties of biomass precursors, the preparation process, and the microstructure of the resulting hard carbon.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, almond (Badam) shell was chosen as the precursor since its components are not specific, the differences in the content of cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin are relatively minor. Through a two-step acid treatment process, enhancements in the cellulose crystallinity of almond shells were observed alongside a reduction in the hemicellulose content. This method concurrently refines and modulates the characteristics of the precursor, streamlining the material preparation process. Subsequent pre-carbonization and high-temperature heat treatments efficiently diminish the specific surface area while introducing more oxygen-containing groups and larger closed pores into the almond shell-derived hard carbon material. The adjustments of the pre-carbonization process hold the potential to abbreviate the equipment's dwell time in the subsequent high-temperature stage, thereby reducing energy consumption. The resulting material assembles the advantages of large interlayer spacing, small specific surface area, abundant closed pores, and oxygen-containing functional groups, which jointly enhance the capacity of the plateau and slope regions with less irreversible capacity loss. In addition, the kinetic and structural evolution of the material during the charging and discharging process is analyzed, and it is shown that it stores sodium ions <span class="italic">via</span> the adsorption-intercalation-filling mechanism. Under the synergistic effect of adsorption, intercalation, and pore-filling sites, the optimized material exhibits high reversible sodium storage capacity and excellent rate performance. This study establishes a link among the properties of biomass precursor, preparation process, hard carbon microstructure, and sodium storage mechanism, which can guide the efficient preparation of biomass-derived hard carbon materials.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect311"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect313"><span class="b_heading">Structure of almond shell-derived hard carbon</span></h3>
        <span>The structural characteristics of hard carbon materials are determined by the nature of the precursor and the carbonization conditions, which significantly affect the electrochemical properties.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16,19,26,27,31</span></sup></a> Therefore, it is essential to modify the precursor through appropriate treatments and control the carbonization conditions. The crystalline cellulose content of biomass determines the cellulose crystallinity, which is closely linked to the structural characteristics of hard carbon materials derived from biomass.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> In this work, a two-step acid treatment was implemented to regulate the cellulose crystallinity and amorphous fraction within almond shells. Furthermore, a two-stage heat treatment approach was utilized to control the microstructure of the resultant hard carbon material. The synthesis of almond shell-derived hard carbon is illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>. In plant cell walls, the fibrous cellulose plays a vital role in maintaining the structure. It is surrounded by hemicellulose and lignin. Cellulose comprises two parts, a crystalline region and an amorphous region. In the amorphous region, the microfibrils are distorted due to internal strain in the fiber, which causes them to tilt and twist.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> When cellulose is subjected to acid hydrolysis, the amorphous region is hydrolyzed more easily than the crystalline region. This is because the compactness of the crystalline region, which is formed by a regular arrangement of fibers, makes it difficult for chemical reagents to penetrate.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a> displays the Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) spectra of samples that underwent acid treatment. As the concentration of hydrochloric acid increased during the second step of the acid treatment, it affected the peak at 1240 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>, corresponding to the stretching vibration of COC in lignin.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> Additionally, the peak at 1740 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>, which denotes the stretching vibration of C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O in hemicellulose and lignin,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> weakened after acid treatment. These changes indicate that the content of hemicellulose and lignin has been altered. The intensity of the peak located at 2900 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> increased, and the hydroxyl peak at around 3400 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> shifted to the low wavenumber direction for A-2.25-6. This indicates the enhancement of hydrogen bonding and an increasing in cellulose crystallinity.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37,38</span></sup></a> Conversely, the peak located at 897 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> corresponds to the -glycosidic bond of A-2.25-10,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> almost disappeared. This suggests that maintaining a moderate acid concentration during the second step of the acid treatment is crucial. Excessive acidity may lead to the hydrolysis of both the amorphous and crystalline segments of cellulose. The data obtained from the component analysis of the samples presented in Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> indicates that the two-step acid treatment effectively decreases the hemicellulose content. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were recorded to further demonstrate changes in the cellulose crystallinity of A-2.25, A-2.25-6, and A-2.25-10. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>, the crystallinity index (CrI) was calculated according to Segal<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a><span class="italic">et al.</span> as provided in the following formula:<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgugt1"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-t1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td><a id="ugt1"><span id="ugt1"></span></a></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>where <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>002</sub></small> is the maximum intensity of the (002) diffracted surface, and <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>am</sub></small> is the minimum intensity around 2<span class="italic"></span> = 18, corresponding to the amorphous region. It is evident from the calculations that A-2.25-6 has the highest cellulose crystallinity after eliminating the amorphous region of cellulose and hemicellulose partially. However, the crystallinity of A-2.25-10 slightly decreases, potentially attributable to the degradation of crystalline cellulose caused by elevated acid concentration.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic illustration of the synthesis procedure.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) FT-IR spectra and (b) XRD patterns of uncarbonized almond shell, A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, (c) XRD patterns, (d) Raman spectra, (e) nitrogen adsorptiondesorption isothermal curves and (f) PSD curves of carbonized A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, A-2.25-6-T, SEM and corresponding high-resolution TEM images of (g) A-2.25, (h) A-2.25-6, (i) A.2.2510, (j) A-2.25-6-T. The inset shows the corresponding SAED patterns and interlayer spacing.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The microcrystalline structure of carbonized samples was analyzed by XRD, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a>, all samples have two broad peaks at approximately 23 and 43, corresponding to the (002) and (100) crystalline plane of graphite microcrystalline domains, which is a characteristic feature of the disordered carbonaceous structure.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> The value of <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>002</sub></small> was calculated according to Bragg's formula and summarized in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. Besides, the graphite-like nanodomain's average lateral width and thickness, <span class="italic">L</span><small><sub>a</sub></small> and <span class="italic">L</span><small><sub>c</sub></small>, were estimated using the Scherrer equation, which describes the microcrystalline structure of hard carbon. The results are also presented in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. The interlayer spacing of samples reduced from A-2.25 (0.393 nm) to A-2.25-6 (0.388 nm) and slightly increased to A-2.25-10 (0.389 nm). The size of the graphite-like nanodomain also shows a trend of increasing and decreasing, which confirms the enhanced graphitization of A-2.25-6 with increased cellulose crystallinity. Upon applying a two-stage pre-carbonization process, A-2.25-6-T demonstrates an increase in disorder, probably due to the shorter residence time at 600 C. Consequently, this abbreviated duration restricts the reorganization of carbon atoms and the parallel transition of carbon layers. Notably, the interlayer spacing of all samples surpasses the 0.37 nm threshold, indicating the facile conquering of the energy barrier by Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and subsequent intercalation into the graphitic domains.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> To distinguish different crystal regions, the XRD patterns were analyzed by profile fitting the broadened (002) diffraction peak.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> As seen in Fig. S3,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> A-2.25-6 has the highest area proportion of the graphitic region. The <span class="italic">R</span>-factor is obtained by comparing the intensity of the (002) peak (B) with the background intensity (A), according to the test methods reported in the literature.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> It measures the number of carbon sheets arranged as single layers. The smaller the R-factor, the higher the carbon content of the monolayer. As shown in Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> and <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>, A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-10 have relatively higher R-factor. This indicates again that the degree of graphitization of carbon materials is significantly affected by the cellulose crystallinity of the precursor.</p>

          <hr class="hrule"><br>
        <p class="otherpara">Raman spectra were used to evaluate the change in carbon structure of all samples. Two characteristic peaks, the D band at around 1350 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> and the G band at around 1585 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>, correspond to the disordered and graphitic structure. As shown in Fig. S4,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the Raman spectra can be deconvoluted into four subpeaks: D peak at approximately 1350 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (corresponding to A<small><sub>1g</sub></small> breathing mode of disordered graphite lattice), <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>3</sub></small> peak at 1500 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (caused by the organic molecule or amorphous structure), G peak at 1585 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (related with E<small><sub>2g</sub></small> stretching vibration mode of graphite lattice), <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> peak at 1620 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (contributed by few layers graphene).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44,45</span></sup></a> Based on the integral area of each subpeak, the D to G peak (<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small>) ratio was calculated to evaluate the disorder of all samples. The <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> value increased from A-2.25 (1.94) to A-2.25-6 (1.76) and then decreased to A-2.25-10 (1.87), which is consistent with the XRD results. This indicates that the two-step acid treatment under suitable conditions effectively enhances the cellulose crystallinity of the precursor, thereby obtaining a hard carbon material with a more ordered structure. In addition, the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> value of A-2.25-6-T exceeds that of A-2.25-6, suggesting a marked influence of pre-carbonization conditions on the microstructure of hard carbon materials.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The N<small><sub>2</sub></small> absorption/desorption isotherms were used to analyze the specific surface area and open pore structure of samples. The adsorption/desorption isotherms (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a>) exhibit a hybrid feature of type-I and type-IV isotherm for A-2.25, A-2.25-6 and a hybrid feature of type-I and type-I isotherm for A-2.25-10, A-2.25-6-T. This suggests that A-2.25-10 and A-2.25-6-T have relatively small external surfaces and microporous structures. On the other hand, micropores and mesopores coexist in A-2.25 and A-2.25-6. Based on the data presented in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>, the BET surface area varies among the samples A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, and A-2.25-6-T, with values of 62.29, 48.17, 0.54, and 1.66 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. The pore widths of A-2.25 and A-2.25-6 are mainly centered at 0.60.8 nm and 1.52.5 nm, with corresponding pore volumes of 0.037 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 0.027 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. In contrast, A-2.25-10 and A-2.25-6-T exhibit minimal pore presence, reflected in their lower pore volumes of 0.0023 and 0.0055 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. The variances in the pore structures observed in A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, and A-2.25-6-T can be attributed to the two-step acid treatment process, which effectively diminished the hemicellulose content within the precursor. This reduction of hemicellulose, a component rich in hydroxyl groups, leads to fewer micropores resulting from the emission of volatile gases during pyrolysis. A diminished BET surface area suggests a reduced accessible surface area for the electrolyte, with fewer sites available for the entrapment of sodium ions for subsequent conversion to the SEI, heightening the ICE. Moreover, it is noteworthy that the specific surface area of A-2.25-6-T demonstrates a significant reduction compared to A-2.25-6, an aspect that will be further analyzed in Section 3.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) were used to characterize the morphology and microstructure of all samples. The carbonized sample has an irregular lamellar morphology with multiple pores approximately 2 m in diameter, as illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2gj</a>. The morphology closely resembles that of the uncarbonized precursor (Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), albeit with notable variations in the diameter and depth of surface pores after carbonization. These alterations can be attributed to the removal of impurities and volatiles during the purification and carbonization process, respectively. As depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1gj</a>, the surface porosity of A-2.25-10 and A-2.25-6-T is notably reduced compared to A-2.25 and A-2.25-6, consistent with the findings of the BET specific surface area analyses. Additionally, all samples exhibit a combination of long-range disorder and short-range order in their turbostratic microstructures, as revealed in HRTEM images. With an increase in the cellulose crystallinity of the precursors, a shift from a disordered to an ordered structure is observed in the resulting samples. Specifically, A-2.25-6 exhibits an abundance of long graphite-like microcrystal stripes (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2h</a>), accompanied by sharper and dispersed diffraction rings in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern. This structural characteristic is attributed to the orderly and compact arrangement of crystalline cellulose, facilitating the preservation of the skeletal framework during pyrolysis. Closed pores are formed by the folding and cross-linking of curved carbon layers, and the merging of nanopores. Consequently, the increased presence of carbon layers within A-2.25-6 engenders more closed pores in comparison to other samples. Intriguingly, the closed pores manifest in A-2.25-6-T exhibit distinct characteristics, featuring thinner pore walls and expanded diameters, attributable to the wider interlayer spacing and reduced layer stacking observed within the structure.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To further clarify the pore structure information of the samples, small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) test was conducted. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>, the SAXS profile consists of inclined straight lines and humps, with three components contributing to its intensity. The inclined lines at a low <span class="italic">Q</span> region were contributed by the scattering of sharp interfaces, corresponding to the macroscopic surface area of the powder grains. The scattering signal in the intermediate <span class="italic">Q</span> range was caused by the micropores in the hard carbon matrix, while the scattering at the higher <span class="italic">Q</span> region is related to the structure of carbon materials.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> The SAXS profiles in the range of 0.010.2 <small><sup>1</sup></small> indicate that the scattering caused by micropores of A-2.25, A-2.25-6, and A-2.25-6-T are more intense than that of A-2.25-10. Specifically, the plateaus around 0.1 <small><sup>1</sup></small> are more distinct in A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T compared to A-2.25, indicating larger closed pore apertures.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> Furthermore, the 2D-SAXS patterns (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3be</a>) demonstrate that all samples contain isotropic pores. From the difference in brightness and intensity of these four patterns, it can be seen that the signal of closed pores in A-2.25-10 is the weakest, suggesting that proper retention of amorphous components in the precursor is favorable for the formation of closed pores.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> The SAXS data was analyzed through the model proposed by Stevens and Dahn,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a> and the diameter of closed pores was fitted as depicted in Fig. S6.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a> shows that A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T have relatively larger closed pores, which is consistent with the TEM results. This means more sodium ions can be stored in A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T, exhibiting higher plateau sodium storage capacity. By integrating the <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>002</sub></small> value from XRD, it can be inferred that A-2.25-6-T, characterized by expanded carbon interlayer spacing and ample closed pores, is poised to deliver exceptional sodium storage capacity and impressive rate performance.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) SAXS profiles of A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, and A-2.25-6-T, (be) 2D-SAXS patterns of different samples, (f) comparison of interlayer distance derived from XRD and pore diameters from SAXS for different samples.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect531"><span class="b_heading">Structural evolution mechanism</span></h3>
        <span>To gain deeper insights into the distinctive pyrolysis behavior and microstructure of A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T arising from variations in pre-carbonation conditions, the precursors and intermediates obtained by pre-carbonation were analyzed through <span class="italic">in situ</span> thermogravimetric analysis coupled with mass spectrometry (TG-MS), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and N<small><sub>2</sub></small> absorption/desorption isotherms measurements. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a> displays the TG-DTG curve of A-2.25-6, revealing three distinct regions characterized by varying rates of relative mass change. Below 130 C, water molecules adsorbed on the precursor surface are removed. A substantial mass loss occurs in the temperature range of 130350 C, attributed to the decomposition, dehydration, decarbonylation, and decarboxylation of organic compounds. Upon surpassing 350 C, the process advances into dehydrogenation, polycondensation, and aromatization of the carbonaceous intermediates, leading to a deceleration in mass loss at this stage.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) TG-DTG curves of A-2.25-6, (b) TG-MS 3D-waterfall plot of A-2.25-6 pyrolyzed at 5 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 1 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small> ramp rates, (c) TG-MS curves, N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorptiondesorption isothermal curves of pre-carbonized A-2.25-6: (d) A-400-1, A-400-5 and A-400-1-1, (e) A-600-5, A-600-1, (f) X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) O 1 s spectra of A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T, (g) schematic illustration of structural evolution of A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The TG-MS plots of released gases during pyrolysis are demonstrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b and c</a>. At temperatures below 400 C, the precursors undergo a decomposition reaction, which produces gases such as CO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, CH<small><sub>4</sub></small>, CO, and a minor amount of H<small><sub>2</sub></small> due to the thermal scission of -glycosidic, COC, OH, COH, C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O, and CH. Interestingly, the pyrolysis of A-2.25-6 generates the same type of gas under both 5 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 1 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small> ramp rates, yet the quantity of gas released differs significantly. A slower ramp rate promotes a more thorough pyrolysis reaction, resulting in a gradual release of gas throughout the process. Conversely, a faster ramp rate initially leads to a delayed gas release, followed by a sudden increase in gas emission once the temperature exceeds 225 C or 325 C. In addition, A-2.25-6-T undergoes an additional hour of pre-carbonization at 400 C, further enhancing the gas evolution during pyrolysis. Based on the above analysis, it can be inferred that A-2.25-6-T releases more gas during the pyrolysis process compared to A-2.25-6.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d and e</a> shows the N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorptiondesorption isothermal curves of carbonaceous intermediates obtained by pre-carbonization of A-2.25-6. The BET surface area of A-400-1 (ramp up to 400 C at 1 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>), A-400-5 (ramp up to 400 C at 5 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and A-400-1-1 (ramp up to 400 C at 1 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small> and hold for 1 h) is 2.75 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, 2.43 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, and 4.83 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Besides, the average pore sizes of A-400-1 and A-400-5 are 7.15 nm and 8.09 nm, respectively, likely attributed to the rapid and substantial release of pyrolysis gases from A-400-5 within the 325400 C temperature range. Following an hour dwell at 400 C, the pore volume of A-400-1-1 increased from 0.0049 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> of A-400-1 to 0.0060 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, markedly larger than the 0.0049 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> of A-400-5. Upon completing the pre-carbonization, the specific surface area of the resulting carbonaceous intermediates increased significantly to 506.84 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 502.43 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for A-600-1 (ramp up to 400 C at 1 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small> and hold for 1 h, then ramp up to 600 C at 5 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and A-600-5 (ramp up to 600 C at 5 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>), respectively. The pore volume of A-600-1 remains substantial at 0.22 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, surpassing that of A-600-5. Notably, A-600-1 predominantly consists of micropores, with micropore and mesopore volumes of 0.19 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 0.028 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. This comprehensive analysis indicates that the two-stage pre-carbonization of gradual temperature increase below 400 C, a dwell at 400 C, and subsequent temperature rise facilitates the slow yet steady release of pyrolysis gases throughout the entire process. This results in the carbonaceous intermediates obtained from the pre-carbonization with a large specific surface area and enriched microporosity.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Upon completion of high-temperature heat treatment, A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T exhibit different BET specific surface areas of 48.17 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 1.66 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Additionally, the pore volume of A-2.25-6-T reduced from 0.22 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> in A-600-1 to 0.0055 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. In contrast, A-2.25-6 still contains many micropores, with a pore volume of 0.027 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. According to previous research,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49</span></sup></a> open micropores tend to close gradually due to the reorganization of carbon atoms and carbon radicals filling in the holes to repair the carbon structure at high temperatures. During the pre-carbonization process, the volatiles of A-2.25-6-T are fully released, providing more time for the closure of micropores during the high-temperature heat treatment stage. Besides, the content of residual oxygen atoms in A-2.25-6-T is slightly higher than that in A-2.25-6, according to the XPS (Table S2) and FT-IR (Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) results. XPS O 1s spectra of A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4f</a>, and they can be deconvoluted into two peaks at 531.8 eV and 533.2 eV, corresponding to C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O and CO, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45,50</span></sup></a> It can be seen that A-2.25-6-T has a higher percentage of C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O among its oxygen-containing functional groups, which is an active site capable of storing sodium ions reversibly (C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O + Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + e<small><sup></sup></small>  CONa).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51</span></sup></a> It is further confirmed by the FT-IR characterizations that A-2.25-6-T exhibits exclusive peaks located at 1720 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 1260 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>, corresponding to the stretching vibration of O<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">COH.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a> This suggests that A-2.25-6-T may possess a superior sodium storage capacity compared to other materials.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Based on the above analysis, the mechanism of structural evolution of A-2.25-6 and A-2.25-6-T (schematically illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4g</a>) can be summarized as follows. During the initial stage of pre-carbonization, the differential heating rates exert a profound influence on the thermally induced decomposition of the precursor. In the case of A-2.25-6-T, the slower heating rate facilitates the complete release of gases like CO, CO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, and CH<small><sub>4</sub></small>, fostering the formation of abundant micropores within the carbonaceous intermediate. Conversely, in A-2.25-6, the release of pyrolysis gases occurs substantially only upon surpassing the threshold temperature of 325 C. This disparity leads to the retention of some volatiles within the intermediates, as reflected by the restricted pore volume at the end of pre-carbonization. After the high-temperature heat treatment, a notable proportion of the micropores within A-2.25-6-T get closed, as the carbon atoms get more time to rearrange. This results in the repair of the carbon structure, significantly reducing the specific surface area. Moreover, A-2.25-6-T has a higher residual oxygen content, which can be attributed to the two-stage pre-carbonization with a residence time at 400 C and a shorter residence time at 600 C. Oxygen atoms in the carbon network have an inhibitory effect on the structural ordering transition, resulting in a relatively disordered structure of A-2.25-6-T with a large interlayer spacing (<a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>).</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect607"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical performance</span></h3>
        <span>To investigate the electrochemical performance of samples and elaborate the relationship between structure and sodium storage capacity, cyclic voltammetry (CV), galvanostatic discharge/charge, rate capability, and cycling stability measurements were conducted. The CV curves of all samples were recorded at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> with a voltage range of 0.012 V (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a>). During the cathodic/anodic scanning processes, a pair of sharp and symmetrical peaks at around 0.01/0.1 V appear, due to the reversible insertion/extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> between the layers of graphite microcrystal. Additionally, a broad cathodic peak at around 0.85 V can be attributed to the formation of SEI on the surface of the electrode, which is caused by the decomposition of the electrolyte.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit52"><sup><span class="sup_ref">52</span></sup></a> After the first cycle, the CV curves of all samples almost overlap (Fig. S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), indicating the reversibility of the reaction between Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and carbon layers. Notably, the irreversible cathodic peak of A-2.25-6-T is the least visible among all the samples, indicating that fewer irreversible reduction reactions occur at the electrode surface, corresponding to a higher ICE. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a> demonstrates the discharge/charge curves of all samples. It can be divided into plateau region and slope region according to the voltage below and above 0.1 V, which is the typical sodium storage behavior of hard carbon.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit53"><sup><span class="sup_ref">53</span></sup></a> Among these samples, A-2.25-6-T delivers the highest initial reversible capacity of 342.4 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with an ICE of 87.19% in comparison to A-2.25 (276.8 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, 79.83%), A-2.25-6 (307.5 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, 84.38%), and A-2.25-10 (292.8 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, 83.18%). The increased reversible capacity and ICE of A-2.25-6-T are likely due to the abundant C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O groups, large closed pores (<span class="italic">R</span> = 0.89 nm), and low specific area (1.66 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). The proportion of plateau region and sloping region capacity of all samples during the second discharge process are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a>. A-2.25-6 with more crystalline cellulose has a more ordered graphite microcrystalline structure containing a higher percentage of the graphitic region compared to A-2.25 and A-2.25-10, and thus, the plateau region capacity increases from 188.5 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 202.4 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for A-2.25 and A-2.25-10, respectively, to 206.7 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for A-2.25-6. Besides, the plateau and slope region capacities of A-2.25-6-T are 127.2 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 229.9 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively, higher than those of A-2.25-6. It can be attributed to its structure with abundant C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O functional groups, expanded carbon layer spacing, and larger closed pores, which provide considerable sodium storage sites. Based on the above results, it can be inferred that the slope region capacity is mainly determined by the content of graphite microcrystals, surface functional groups, and defects. In contrast, the plateau region capacity is primarily affected by the size of the graphite microcrystals, the spacing of the carbon interlayers, and the structure of the closed pores, and the ICE is related to the specific surface area and the structural order of hard carbon materials. Controlled by different structural properties of hard carbon, capacity contributed by slope and plateau regions has its pros and cons. The voltage in the plateau region is below 0.1 V, and as the low discharge plateau of anode materials favors the increase of the output voltage, the full cell will exhibit a higher energy density.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> However, slower kinetics will lead to limited rate performance as the sodium storage behaviors in the plateau region are controlled by diffusion processes. In contrast, the sodium storage behaviors in the slope region have faster kinetics and better rate performance. However, the percentage of slope region should not be too high. Otherwise, too many irreversible reactions will lead to low ICE.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">Electrochemical performance characterization of A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, and A-2.25-6-T. (a) CV curves of the first cycle, (b) initial galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles at 0.03 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, (c) the plateau and sloping capacity distribution below and above 0.1 V, (d) rate performance, (e) galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles of A-2.25-6-T at different current densities, (f) Nyquist plots of A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, and A-2.25-6-T before cycling, (g) cycle performance at 0.3 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, (h) comparison of rate capability between A-2.25-6-T in this work and recently reported hard carbon anode.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a> illustrates the rate capacities of all samples. It shows that A-2.25-6-T has a remarkable rate performance. The discharge/charge curves of A-2.25-6-T at different current densities are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e</a>. At the current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, A-2.25-6-T delivers the reversible capacities of 309.9, 294.8, 277.6, 253.5, 235.4 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Even under higher current densities of 5 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 10 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, it maintains impressive capacities of 189.6 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 128.3 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Notably, these values surpass those reported for most hard carbon anode materials,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18,5459</span></sup></a> as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5h</a> and Table S5.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> To assess the sodium storage kinetics of various samples employed as anodes in SiBs, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted. As demonstrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5f</a>, all Nyquist plots consist of a semicircle in the high-frequency region and inclined straight lines in the low-frequency region, corresponding to charge transfer resistance and Warburg impedance in the diffusion process, respectively. Notably, the Nyquist plot of A-2.25-6-T exhibits a small semicircle indicative of a charge transfer impedance that is within 5 . This suggests that the expanded interlayer spacing and large closed pores enhance the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> electrochemical reaction kinetics during the sodiation process,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">60,61</span></sup></a> resulting in improved rate performance of the material.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The long-term cycling performance of all samples at 0.3 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5g</a>. After 400 cycles, the A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, and A-2.25-6-T still exhibit reversible capacities of 215.6, 249.8, 242.5, 282.5 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, with an average CE of 99.8%. The corresponding capacity retention is 84.8%, 87.3%, 89.7%, and 88.5%, respectively. The slower capacity decay rate of A-2.25-6 compared to A-2.25 suggests that the ordered structure with fewer defects is more structurally stable during charging and discharging. In addition, the noteworthy capacity retention exhibited by A-2.25-10 and A-2.25-6-T implies that the SEI formed on the surface of these particles, characterized by smaller specific surface areas and non-porous or less porous nature is notably more stable. This enhanced stability ultimately reduces the irreversible loss of sodium ions throughout the charge/discharge cycling process. Although there was some variation in the capacity retention of the four samples, overall, they all remained at relatively high levels. It can be attributed to the turbostratic structure of as-prepared hard carbon where graphitic-like and highly disordered regions coexist. The large interlayer spacing allows sodium ions to be repeatedly intercalated and de-intercalated between the layers without altering the microstructure of the hard carbon, and the suitable degree of graphitization allows sodium ions to be reversibly absorbed and de-absorbed at defective sites and functional groups without generating a large amount of irreversible capacity loss.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To further evaluate the practicability of the resulting hard carbon materials, the full cell was constructed by using A-2.25-6-T anode and commercial Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V2(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> (NVP) cathode, denoted as A-2.25-6-T//NVP. As illustrated in Fig. S12a,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the A-2.25-6-T//NVP delivers an initial discharge capacity of 113.7 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 20 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with an ICE of 95.4%. Moreover, Fig. S12b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> shows the rate capability of A-2.25-6-T//NVP. At the current densities of 0.02, 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the full cell exhibits an average specific capacity of 119.2, 114.0, 99.7, 74.1, 56.9, 25.3, and 29.1 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, the corresponding power density and energy density of the full cell are shown in Fig. S12d.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> At the power density of 42.6 W kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the full cell exhibits a maximal energy density of 255.6 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Even at a power density of 1071.4 W kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the full cell still maintains an energy density of 53.6 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>, indicating the potential application prospects of A-2.25-6-T in the SiBs.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect671"><span class="b_heading">Sodium storage mechanism</span></h3>
        <span>To further analyze the sodium storage behaviors, all samples were evaluated using CV at different scan rates. The Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion coefficient (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small>) was determined utilizing the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). The CV curves obtained at scan rates varying from 0.2 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> to 3 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a</a> and S10(ac).<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> It is evident that the redox peak current has a positive correlation with the scan rate,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit62"><sup><span class="sup_ref">62</span></sup></a> as elucidated by the following formula:<br><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">a</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small></td></tr></tbody></table>In the given equation, <span class="italic">a</span> and <span class="italic">b</span> are constants, and the value of <span class="italic">b</span> is determined by the slope of the linear plot of log(<span class="italic">i</span>)log(<span class="italic"></span>). Normally, the value of <span class="italic">b</span> serves as an indicator of electrochemical behaviors. A <span class="italic">b</span> value nearing 0.5 signifies a diffusion-controlled electrochemical behavior in the electrode. Whereas the <span class="italic">b</span> value around 1 represents the surface-controlled capacitive process. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b</a>, the <span class="italic">b</span> values of cathodic peaks are 0.67, 0.56, 0.65, and 0.62 for A-2.25, A-2.25-6, A-2.25-10, and A-2.25-6-T, respectively. This indicates that diffusion and capacitive behaviors coexist in the sodium ion storage process, while the diffusion process is dominant. Besides, the above formula can be converted to another form,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit63"><sup><span class="sup_ref">63</span></sup></a> where the <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> are constants. The value of <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> can be obtained by linear fitting the <span class="italic">i</span>(<span class="italic">V</span>)/<span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> and <span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small>. Furthermore, the contribution of surface-capacitive and diffusion-controlled processes are determined by <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic"></span> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small>, respectively.<br><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText"><span class="italic">i</span>(<span class="italic">V</span>)/<span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> = <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> + <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small></td></tr></tbody></table></span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) CV curves of A-2.25-6-T at various scan rates from 0.2 to 3.0 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>, (b) linearity relationship between log(<span class="italic">i</span><small><sub>p</sub></small>) and log(<span class="italic"></span>) of cathodic peaks, (c) CV curve of A-2.25-6-T at the scan rate of 3 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The shaded region shows the calculated capacitive contribution, (d) the contribution ratios of capacitive capacity at different scan rates, (eh) galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) potential profiles and the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion coefficients calculated from the GITT potential profiles of A-2.25-6-T during discharge process (e and g) and charge process (f and h).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The calculation results are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6d</a>. It is noteworthy that A-2.25-6 has the lowest capacitive contribution whereas A-2.25-6-T and A-2.25 have relatively higher values. It can be attributed to the presence of fewer defect sites and a higher abundance of graphite microcrystals in A-2.25-6, offering numerous diffusion channels for sodium ions. Consequently, the sodium storage behavior of A-2.25-6 is predominated by diffusion-controlled mechanisms, with interlayer intercalation and pore filling playing vital roles. As abundant defects and oxygen-containing functional groups in A-2.25 and A-2.25-6-T provide ample sodium ion adsorption sites, the surface-controlled processes in their sodium storage behavior are more pronounced.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The GITT potential profiles of A-2.25-6-T and other samples are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6eh</a> and S11.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> The corresponding <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> was calculated by the following formula according to Fick's second law.<br></p><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgugt4"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-t4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-t4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-t4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td><a id="ugt4"><span id="ugt4"></span></a></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>where <span class="italic"></span> (s) is the pulse duration, <span class="italic">m</span><small><sub>B</sub></small> (g), <span class="italic">V</span><small><sub>m</sub></small> (cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> mol<small><sup>1</sup></small>), <span class="italic">M</span><small><sub>B</sub></small> (g mol<small><sup>1</sup></small>), <span class="italic">S</span> (m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) is the active mass, molar volume, molecular weight, and surface area of the electrode, respectively. <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>S</sub></small> and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub></sub></small> are the potential changes caused by pulse and constant current discharging/charging. Based on the variation of <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small>, the sodium storage behavior can be divided into three regions. During the discharge process (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6g</a>), <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> exhibits an elevation at a voltage exceeding 0.5 V, corresponding to the rapid adsorption of sodium ions onto the carbon interface. In a continuous sodiation process, the surface sites are gradually occupied, and the repulsion between sodium ions is increased, leading to a precipitous decline in <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> within the voltage interval of 0.5 V to 0.3 V. In the second sodium storage range of 0.30.1 V, <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> shows a rebound, which can be attributed to the interaction of sodium ions with C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O functional groups. Lastly, the sub-0.1 V region, characterized by a discharge plateau in the voltage curve, shows an initial decline in <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> followed by an upsurge, corresponding to the intercalation of sodium ions within the carbon layer and the filling of micropores, respectively. During desodiation, <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> shows a temporal diminishing and subsequently augmenting trend within the initial stages and an opposite trend within the conclusive stages, while decreasing within the second stage. In the first region, sodium ions leave the carbon layers initially and are then removed from the micropores <span class="italic">via</span> channels provided by graphite microcrystals.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit64"><sup><span class="sup_ref">64</span></sup></a> Subsequent regions correspond to the disodiation of C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O groups and other surface adsorption sites. Additionally, <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> of A-2.25-6-T surpasses that of A-2.25-6 in the voltage range below 0.1 V and above 1 V during the charging process. This phenomenon suggests that heightened structural disorder and larger closed pores contribute to enhanced sodium ion mobility, resulting in the superior rate performance exhibited by A-2.25-6-T.<p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">The structural evolution of an electrode derived from A-2.25-6-T during the discharge/charge process was thoroughly examined using <span class="italic">ex situ</span> Raman spectra and XRD patterns at various voltages, as illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a>. Notably, the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> ratio exhibits a gradual decrease above 0.3 V, attributed to the absorption of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions at defect sites and edge of the carbon layers, leading to the weakening of the D-band,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> as depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7b</a>. Upon discharging to 0.1 V, a marginal redshift in the G-band and a rebound in the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> ratio are observed, likely stemming from the interaction of sodium ions with the C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O groups. Subsequently, the ratio of <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> decreases again within the voltage range of 0.10.01 V. During the charging process, the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> ratio gradually reverts to the level before discharging, indicating a high reversibility of the sodium storage reaction. As illustrated in the <span class="italic">ex situ</span> XRD patterns presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7c</a>, the position of the (002) peak remains relatively stable throughout the discharge process, indicating that the large interlayer spacing of A-2.25-6-T can accommodate the variations in spacing caused by the intercalation of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. Furthermore, the characteristic peak of sodium metal appears at 0.1 V and becomes more pronounced at 0.01 V.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> It can also be seen in the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a> that the color of the ethanol solution gradually turns red, providing evidence of some Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> fill in the closed pores and gradually transforming into quasi-metallic Na.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta03284e-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta03284e/d4ta03284e-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Selected voltage sites in the discharge/charge profiles for <span class="italic">ex situ</span> measurement. The inset shows the color change of ethanol solution containing 1% phenolphthalein after reaction with A-2.25-6-T electrode for 5 min, <span class="italic">ex situ</span> Raman spectra (b), and <span class="italic">ex situ</span> XRD patterns (c) of A-2.25-6-T at various voltages, (d) schematic diagram of sodium ion storage mechanism at different discharge stages.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Based on the above analysis, it is concluded that the sodium storage mechanism of the A-2.25-6-T anode can be elucidated through a three-stage model: adsorption-intercalation-pore filling. As illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7d</a>, the process begins with the primary adsorption of sodium ions on defect sites and surface functional groups within the high-voltage slope region (OCV-0.3 V), followed by reactions with C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O groups within the 0.30.1 V range. Ultimately, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> intercalates into the carbon layers of graphite microcrystal, fills in closed pores, and undergoes further reduction to form quasi-metallic sodium clusters within the low-voltage plateau region.</p>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect823"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, we have successfully developed a simple and scalable strategy for synthesizing hard carbon derived from almond shells for efficient sodium ion storage. This approach involves a sequential two-step acid treatment followed by a two-stage heat treatment process. The acid treatment of almond shells enhances cellulose crystallinity and reduces hemicellulose content by controlling acid concentration. Furthermore, the microstructure of the resulting hard carbon material is tailored by introducing an additional dwell time at intermediate temperatures and reducing the heating rate during the pre-carbonization stage. Through the synergistic effects of acid treatment and pre-carbonization, the optimized sample A-2.25-6-T exhibits an expanded interlayer spacing, plentiful C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O functional groups, closed pores, and a specific surface area as low as 1.66 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. These structural features render the A-2.25-6-T anode to achieve a notable specific capacity of 342.4 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 30 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, with an ICE of 87.19%. Moreover, the A-2.25-6-T anode exhibits a superior rate performance with a reversible capacity of 128.3 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 10 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Besides, our investigation supports the hypothesis of an adsorption-intercalation-pore filling mechanism for sodium storage in the A-2.25-6-T anode, as substantiated by CV, GITT, and analyses of ex-situ Raman spectra and XRD patterns. Our findings provide new insight into the intrinsic connection among properties of biomass precursors, preparation process, and microstructure of the hard carbon, thereby providing theoretical guidance for structural designing of high-performance biomass-derived hard carbon materials. We hope that our proposed strategy will promote the development of sustainable materials and foster the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries.</span>
